Addendum


1. General Outline


2. Summary of 2025 Dietary Reference
Intakes for Koreans (KDRI)


3. ｢2020 한국인 영양소 섭취기준｣ 요약표

무기질
1 General Outline
1 Directions for the Establishment and Revision of the KDRI
1-1. Purpose
The Korean Dietary Reference Intakes (KDRI) are standards for energy and nutrient
intakes that are established to help individuals maintain health throughout the life course
while minimizing the risks of both nutrient deficiencies and diet-related chronic diseases.
Accordingly, the directions for the establishment and revision of the KDRI extend beyond
the prevention of nutrient deficiencies resulting from inadequate energy and nutrient
intake to encompass the maintenance of overall health and the reduction of chronic
disease risk across the lifespan.
The 2025 KDRI presents reference values designed to ensure safe and adequate nutrition
for the maintenance and promotion of health—including the Estimated Average
Requirement (EAR), Recommended Nutrient Intake (RNI), Adequate Intake (AI), and
Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL)—as well as reference values that consider the prevention
of diet-related chronic diseases, such as the Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range
(AMDR) and the Chronic Disease Risk Reduction Intake (CDRR). Ultimately, KRDRI aim to
contribute to extending the healthy life expectancy of the Korean population.
1-2. Establishment and Revision Directions of the 2025 KDRI
The establishment and revision of the 2025 KDRI was undertaken with the primary goal
of advancing the level of evidence for each nutrient while strengthening the consistency
and scientific rigor of indicator setting, by reflecting the nutritional status, health
indicators and the latest scientific evidence relevant to the Korean population. To this
end, scientific evidence across multiple levels—including exposure indicators (biomarkers
and functional indicators), health assessment indicators, and health outcomes—was
systematically evaluated, and the indicators appropriate for estimating nutrient
requirements were reorganized for each nutrient. In setting AI, particular attention was

General Outline
given to situations in which the EAR was not feasible. The applicable scope of scientific
evidence was clearly defined, and research limitations-such as insufficient data, uncertain
health outcome responses, and issue of representativeness-were systematically reviewed.
With respect to harmonization of evidence levels, fundamental calculation parameters,
including coefficients of variation and uncertainty factors, were reviewed in comparison
with the latest scientific evidence and international standards. In addition, the estimation
process across life stages (e.g., infants, children, and adolescents) were re-evaluated
based on the frequency and relevance of the supporting literature. The objectives of
literature evaluations were further clarified, leading to re-examinations of the appropriateness
of exposure indicators used for estimating EAR, the need to transition toward health status
indicators, and the currency of evidence used in setting UL, thereby strengthening the
validity of nutrient-specific evidence.
From the perspective of international harmonization, the standard literature framework
based on NUQUEST (NUtrition QUality Evaluation Strengthening Tools) was updated to
establish a consistent and unified evaluation system among domestic researchers.
Furthermore, the most recent DRIs from other countries—including the United States,
Japan, the European Union, Australia, New Zealand, the United Kingdom, and China—
were comparatively analyzed to secure up-to-date, nutrient-specific evidence. Changes in
anthropometric characteristics and dietary intake patterns observed during the COVID-19
pandemic were also taken into consideration. Through these efforts, the public role of the
KDRI was redefined as a comprehensive standard integrating policy, practice, and science.
❙Figure 1❙ Establishment and Revision Directions for 2025 KDRI

무기질
Accordingly, this 2025 KDRI volume includes the scientific evaluation methods
supporting the establishment and revision reference values, the latest scientific evidence
derived from systematic reviews, data on nutrient intake status and major food sources
among the Korean population, and information on international harmonization (Figure
1). The evidence-based revision is officially announced by the Ministry of Health and
Welfare in accordance with the National Nutrition Management Act, with the aim of
supporting the development of national nutrition policies and facilitating their
application and management in both industry and practice so that all citizens can
implement them. Ultimately, this framework is intended to contribute to improvements in
the nutritional status of Koreans and to the extension of healthy life expectancy.
1-3. History of the KDRI Establishment and Revision
The recognition that the deficiency of a specific factor in food could cause disease
was first established in 1905. Over the subsequent century, most essential nutrients were
identified, and the causal relationships between individual nutrients and their associated
deficiency diseases were elucidated [1, 2]. Accordingly, early nutritional guidelines focused
primarily on the prevention of nutrient deficiencies, leading to the establishment of the
Recommended Dietary Allowances (RDAs), defined as the amount of a nutrient required
to avoid deficiency diseases and to maintain normal physiological function and growth


[3]. The first RDAs were announced in 1941 at the U.S. National Nutrition Conference on
Defense and included reference values for energy, protein, calcium, phosphorus, iron,
and vitamins A·C·D, thiamin, riboflavin, and niacin. However, since the late 20th century,
economic development and advances in food processing technologies have markedly
reduced the prevalence of nutrient deficiency. In contrast, concerns regarding nutritional
imbalance and overnutrition have emerged, raising issues related to safety and chronic
disease risk. In response, in 1997, the United States and Canada, through the National
Academy of Sciences, introduced a new framework of nutrient intake standards-the
Dietary Reference Intakes (DRI)-which consider health risks associated with both nutrient
deficiency and excess intake [4].
In Korea, the first Korean RDA were established in 1962 under the leadership of the
FAO Korea Regional Office, covering 10 nutrients (energy, protein, vitamins A·D·C·B ·B ,
1 2
niacin, calcium, and iron). These recommendations were subsequently revised twice, in
1967 and 1975. Further revisions were carried out in 1985 and 1989 under the direction

General Outline
of the Korea Institute for Health and Social Affairs. In 1995 and 2000, the Korean
Nutrition Society led additional revisions, expanding the KDRA to include vitamin E,
vitamin B , folate, phosphorus, and zinc, resulting in dietary recommendations for a total
of 15 nutrients. Around this time, it became evident that health problems related to both
nutrient deficiency and excess coexisted in Korea. While the proportion of individuals
consuming excessive amounts of energy and fat increased, a substantial proportion of the
population continued to consume calcium, iron, riboflavin, and other nutrients below the
EAR. Concurrently, the prevalence of diet-related chronic diseases-such as obesity,
diabetes, and hypercholesterolemia-showed an increasing trend [5]. In addition, since
1999, cancer incidence has risen annually [6]. Amid these changes, the Korean Nutrition
Society expanded the number of covered nutrients from 15 to 34 nutrients in 2005 and
initiated a paradigm shift from RDA to the DRI. The nutrients included comprised energy;
six macronutrients (carbohydrate, lipids, protein, amino acids, dietary fiber, and water);
four fat-soluble vitamins (vitamins A, D, E, and K); nine water-soluble vitamins (vitamin
C, thiamin, riboflavin, niacin, vitamin B , folate, vitamin B , pantothenic acid, and
6 12
biotin); six major minerals (calcium, phosphorus, sodium, chloride, potassium, and
magnesium); and eight trace minerals (iron, zinc, copper, fluoride, manganese, iodine,
selenium, and molybdenum). In 2010, total sugars were added, bringing the total number
of nutrients covered by the KDRI to 35 [7]. In the same year, the National Nutrition
Management Act was promulgated, transferring responsibility for the establishment and
revision of the DRIs from the private sector to the national government. Article 14 (1) of
the Act stipulates that the Minister of Health and Welfare shall establish and regularly
revise DRIs necessary for the promotion of public health and systematically disseminate
them to academia, industry, and relevant institutions. To maintain their applicability and
effectiveness, the DRIs are to be established and revised every five years, reflecting the
latest scientific evidence, changes in the anthropometric characteristics and disease
patterns of the Korean population, and shifts in dietary practices and food environments.
Accordingly, the Ministry of Health and Welfare commissioned the Korean Nutrition
Society to develop the DRIs, In 2015, the first national standard was established and
announced, presenting DRIs for 36 nutrients [8]. Subsequently, in 2020, the second edition
of KDRI was completed and promulgated, covering a total of 40 nutrients (Table 1).

General Outline
2 Establishment and Revision Processes of the KDRI
2-1. Establishment of Reference Values
The Dietary Reference Intakes for Koreans (KDRI) are based on three indicators
intended to prevent inadequate intake-namely, the Estimated Average Requirement (EAR),
Recommended Nutrient Intake (RNI), and Adequate Intake (AI)- as well as the Tolerable
Upper Intake Level (UL), which is designed to prevent adverse health effects resulting
from excessive intake. In addition, the KDRIs include the Chronic Disease Risk Reduction
Intake (CDRR), which links indicators of both inadequate and excessive intake to the risk
of chronic disease (Figure 2). Furthermore, to reduce the risk of chronic diseases
associated with energy imbalance, the Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range
(AMDR) is established for carbohydrate, lipids, and protein.
When sufficient scientific evidence regarding nutrient requirements is available, EAR and
RNI are established; when the evidence is insufficient, AIs are established instead. UL are set
only when scientific evidence regarding adverse effects from excessive intake is available;
however, the absence of a UL does not imply that potential adverse effects can be entirely
excluded. In the 2025 KDRIs, the relationships between nutrients and chronic diseases-such as
cardiovascular disease and hypertension-were reviewed, and for nutrients with sufficiently
robust scientific evidence, the establishment of CDRR was considered. Consequently, some
nutrients have all three values—EAR, RNI, and UL—established, whereas for others, only
selected reference values, such as AI, UL, or CDRR, are provided.
❙Figure 2❙ Establishment of Reference Values

무기질
(1) Estimated Average Requirement (EAR)
The EAR is defined as the value derived from the median of the distribution of daily nutrient
requirements among healthy individuals in a specific age and sex group and represents the
level at which the requirements of approximately half of the population are met. Nutrient
requirements can be estimated when there are functional indicators that respond sensitively to
intake and established criteria for assessing nutritional status. Because functional indicators
have not been identified for all nutrients, it has not been possible to estimate requirements for
certain nutrients. In particular, there are technical limitations in directly measuring individual
energy requirements. Therefore, energy requirements are estimated based on energy
expenditure, under the assumption of energy balance. Consequently, for energy, the term
Estimated Energy Requirement (EER) is used instead of EAR.
(2) Recommended Nutrient Intake (RNI)
The RNI is the intake level that meets the nutrient requirements of approximately 97–
98% of individuals in a given population group and is calculated by adding two standard
deviations (or using the coefficient of variation) to the EAR.
(3) Adequate Intake (AI)
The AI is established when sufficient or appropriate scientific evidence is not available
to set an EAR and the corresponding RNI. It is based on observed or experimentally
determined approximations or estimates of nutrient intake among groups (or multiple
groups) of individuals who are presumed to be healthy. In this context, healthy
individuals are considered to be maintaining an adequate nutritional status [9]. Examples
of adequate nutritional status include normal growth, maintenance of normal body levels
of nutrients, and other indicators of nutritional adequacy and overall health.
The AI represents an intake level that is expected to meet or exceed the requirements
of most individuals within a specific age and sex group. When an RNI cannot be
established, the AI may be used in place of RNI as guidance for average daily intake at
the individual level. However, its application in nutritional status assessment is limited.
(4) Tolerable Upper Intake Level (UL)
The UL is defined as the highest level of daily nutrient intake that is unlikely to pose
a risk of adverse health effects for most healthy individuals. Establishing a UL requires

General Outline
sufficient scientific evidence demonstrating that adverse effects may occur with excessive
intake. The UL is derived based on the No Observed Adverse Effect Level (NOAEL)—the
highest intake level at which no adverse effects are observed—or the Lowest Observed
Adverse Effect Level (LOAEL)—the lowest intake level at which adverse effects have been
observed. An Uncertainty Factor (UF), accounting for inter-individual variability in
sensitivity and other sources of uncertainty, is then applied in the derivation of the UL.
Although the UL serves as a reference value to prevent adverse effects associated with
excessive intake, it does not represent a level that is absolutely safe for all individuals.
In some sensitive subpopulations, adverse effects may occur even at intake levels below
the UL.
UL = NOAEL or LOAEL / UF
(5) Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Range (AMDR)
The AMDR refers to the proportion of total energy intake contributed by carbohydrate,
lipids, and protein. Based on scientific evidence indicating that the relative contributions
of energy-providing nutrients are associated with health outcomes [10, 11], the AMDRs
were established for carbohydrate, lipids, and protein.
The AMDR is expressed as an appropriate range of the proportion of energy intake
derived from each macronutrient relative to total energy intake. The acceptable range for
each macronutrient was determined based on energy intake distributions that are
expected to reduce the risk of chronic diseases and nutritional imbalance while ensuring
adequate intake of other essential nutrients, such as vitamins and minerals. Accordingly,
macronutrient energy intakes that fall outside the specified AMDRs indicate an increased
risk of adverse health outcomes.
(6) Chronic Disease Risk Reduction Intake (CDRR)
The CDRR refers to the level of intake at which the risk of developing chronic disease
can be reduced by modifying the intake of a specific nutrient in a healthy population.
The CDRR can be established only when sufficient scientific evidence is available. Its
establishment involves verifying whether a causal relationship exists between nutrient
intake and chronic disease and, based on analyses of dose-response relationships,
considering a specific intake level or range that is associated with a reduced risk of
chronic disease

무기질
2-2. Life Stage groups
To determine the life stage classifications for the 2025 Dietary Reference Intakes for
Koreans (KDRI), the age groupings used in the 2020 KDRI, national statistical data


[12-17], and large-scale national surveys related to health behaviors and nutrition in
Korea [18-21] were reviewed. In addition, the age classifications used in dietary reference
intakes systems of other countries were examined for comparison [22-27].
To assess the appropriateness of age groupings in relation to physiological
developmental stages and their applicability in practice, the upper limit of the growth
period and the starting age and subdivisions of older adulthood were evaluated. For
adolescents in the growth period, the 2020 KDRI defined the upper age limit as 18 years,
whereas the educational system of the Ministry of Education uses 17 years as the upper
limit. Accordingly, the appropriateness of the upper age boundary for adolescence was
discussed. A comprehensive review of legal and administrative age classifications,
physiological development, applicability across different fields of use, and analysis of
data from the Korea National Health and Nutrition Examination Survey (KNHANES) over
the past five years indicated that inclusion or exclusion of 18-year olds did not result in
meaningful differences. Moreover, clinical perspectives suggesting that growth may not
be fully completed in some individuals at age 18 were considered. Based on these
findings, it was decided to retain the upper age limit for adolescence at 18 years,
consistent with previous standards. To evaluate the appropriateness of age classifications
for older adults, domestic [16,21] and international [22-28] definitions of older age were
reviewed. In addition, analyses of recent KNHANES data comparing nutrient intakes and
the prevalence of chronic disease across various older-age subgroups showed insufficient
scientific evidence to justify changes to the existing age classification. Accordingly, the
starting age and subdivisions for older adulthood will maintained as 65–74 years and 75
years and older, consistent with previous KDRI.
Therefore, the age classifications for the 2025 KDRI remain the same as those in the 2020
KDRI and are based on physiological life stages: infancy (under 1 year), early childhood (1–5
years), childhood (6–11 years), adolescence (12–18 years), adulthood (19–64 years), and older
adulthood (65 years and older) (Table 2). Infancy (under 1 year) was subdivided into two age
groups: 0–5 months and 6–11 months. Early childhood (1–5 years) was also subdivided into
two age groups: 1–2 years and 3–5 years. Sex specific classifications begin at age 6, the start
of childhood. From ages 6 and to 14, age groups were defined in 3-year intervals (6–8, 9–11,
and 12–14 years), with a separate group for ages 15–18 years. Adulthood (19–64 years) was

General Outline
divided into 19–29, 30–49, and 50–64 years. Older adulthood (65 years and older) was
divided into two groups: 65–74 years and 75 years and older.
However, given the continued increase in the proportion of older adults, anticipated
improvements in functional capacity among those currently classified as older adults, and
the growing number of older individuals who live independently despite having chronic
conditions (e.g., hypertension), it will be necessary to review recent WHO perspectives
calling for a redefinition of health in older populations [29]. Accordingly, for the
development of the 2030 KDRI, it will be important to establish a clear definition of health
in older adulthood and to secure robust evidence related to chronic diseases and
functional capacity in older adults, enabling a thorough reassessment of age classifications
for this life stage.

2-3. Anthropometric Standards
Because nutrient requirements vary according to physiological changes across the life
cycle and differences in body size, anthropometric reference values must be considered
when establishing DRIs. Given the substantial interindividual variability in body size,
the general principle is to derive standardized anthropometric reference values by
sex and age group and to use these values as the basis for setting nutrient intake
recommendations. For infants under 1 year of age and growing children and adolescents,
the 2020 KDRI adopted the 2017 Growth Standards for Children and Adolescents [30]. The

무기질
2025 KDRI also applied the same principle and likewise used the 2017 Growth Standards
for Children and Adolescents as the anthropometric reference for both infants under 1
year of age and children and adolescents. For adults, the 2020 KDRI utilized data from the
KNHANES over the most recent 5-year period (2013-2017). Among the healthy adults aged
19-40 years with a body mass index in the range of 18.5-24.9 kg/m2, the median BMI
values (BMI 22.6 for men and 21.4 for women) were applied as anthropometric standards
reflecting the concept of healthy weight. The 2025 KDRI follow the same methodological
framework; however, instead of using the most recent 5-year dataset, analyses were based
on data collected over a 10-year period. To avoid potential bias from atypical changes in
body size associated with COVID-19 pandemic, data from 2020-2022 were excluded, and
only data from 2013-2019 analyzed. As a result, the median BMI values for adults
remained unchanged from the 2020 KDRI, at 22.6 for men and 21.4 for women, and these
values were used to derive the adult anthropometric standards. The anthropometric
standards derived using this approach are presented in Table 3.

General Outline
2-4. Systematic Literature Review
The systematic literature review conducted to revise and update the 2025 KDRI was
performed in a structured and rigorous manner in accordance with a standardized
protocol developed by the research team. This protocol comprised eight sequential steps:
(1) formulation of research questions and development of analytical framework; (2)
establishment of literature selection criteria; (3) literature search; (4) screening of
identified studies; (5) data extraction; (6) assessment of study quality; (7) synthesis and
narrative description of study findings; and (8) evaluation of the level of evidence and
derivation of conclusion. Each step was specified in detail, including the tools and
standardized forms required to ensure consistency and reproducibility. In addition,
researcher-developed evidence summary tables and reference example materials were
provided to facilitate the systematic review process.
Separate analytical frameworks were developed for establishingthe EAR and the UL.
Each framework consisted of four hierarchical components: (1) nutrient sources (e.g.,
foods and supplements); (2) exposure indicators (e.g., concentration of nutrients and
functions of their metabolites); (3) intermediate biomarkers (e.g., blood pressure, blood
lipid concentrations, inflammatory markers); and (4) final health outcomes (e.g., disease
prevalence, incidence, or mortality). These frameworks were conceptualized and
diagrammed by the research team, and completeness of the analyses was enhanced
through the application of investigator-developed checklists specific to the EAR and UL.
Research questions were formulated using the PICO (Patient, Intervention, Comparator,
Outcome) approach, on the basis of which key search terms were identified. These terms
were used to search both domestic and international databases, with the scope of the
search limited to studies conducted in generally healthy populations. Eligible studies were
peer-reviewed journal articles published in Korean or English, and study designs were
restricted to intervention studies, cohort studies, case-control studies, and cross-sectional
studies. During the screening process, the Rayyan program was used for initial title and
abstract screening, followed by full-text review to determine the final set of studies to be
incorporated into the establishment of the dietary reference intakes.
The risk of bias of the finally selected studies were assessed using internationally
validated assessment tools according to study design. In this review, the NUQUEST
(NUtrition QUality Evaluation Strengthening Tools, 2022), a too specifically developed to
address the unique characteristics of nutrition research, was employed [31]. NUQUEST

무기질
was selected to complement the limitations of conventional quality assessment tools and
has demonstrated validity and reliability. Previously published systematic reviews were
assessed using either AMSTAR 2 (A MeaSurement Tool to Assess Systematic Reviews-2) or
ROBIS (Risk of Bias in Systematic Reviews) [32, 33].
The overall strength of scientific evidence was synthesized and evaluated in accordance
with the criteria of the USDA Nutrition Evidence Library, and the level of evidence for
each health outcome was classified into four categories: Strong, Moderate, Limited, and
Grade Not Assignable [34]. To ensure consistent reporting of evidence grading results, the
research team developed and applied an additional set of standardized reporting
templates.
Accordingly, the standardized protocol developed in this study and the systematic
review process conducted on its basis are expected to contribute to the comprehensive
and reliable accumulation of scientific evidence for the establishment of the 2025 KDRI.
2-5. Considerations for Special Population Group
(1) Infants
As in the 2020 KDRI, the 2025 KDRI did not establish separate intake recommendations
for formula-fed infants. For infants aged 0–5 months, nutrient intake recommendations
were expressed as AI derived from data on breast milk intake and nutrient content of
human milk. The reference value for breast-milk intake in infants was set at 780 mL/day,
consistent with values used in major international guidelines. The Steering Committee
determined that, until sufficient domestic data on breast-milk intake among Korean
infants are accumulated, it is appropriate to maintain the same reference volume as that
used internationally. Accordingly, this value was applied without modification in the 2025
KDRI.
For infants aged 6–11 months, nutrient intake recommendations should account for
both breast milk (or formula) and the intake of complementary foods. However, when
evidence required to directly establish nutrient intake criteria for this age group was
insufficient, values were extrapolated from established for infants aged 0–5-months,
taking metabolic body weight into consideration. The calculation formula is presented
below. Because infants aged 0-5 months are in a growth phase and the intake values for

General Outline
this age group are assumed to already incorporate a growth factor, no additional growth
factor was applied in this extrapolation formula.
However, it should be recognized that growth patterns vary even within this age ranges
of 0-5 months and 6-11 months. Therefore, the values presented for each age category
are intended only to represent the respective age range, and it is desirable that they be
applied flexibly according to the infant’s actual growth status.
(2) Growth and Development Period (Children and Adolescents)
When evidence and data required to establish nutrient intake recommendations for
children and adolescents (1–18 years), values were estimated based on the assumption
that nutrient requirements for maintenance are similar between adults and growing
children, with adjustments made for metabolic differences related to body weight.
Because dietary reference values for adults reflect only requirements for maintenance,
factors related to growth must be added when extrapolating these values to children and
adolescents.
Growth factors derived from FAO/WHO/UNU were applied after being adapted to
match the age group classifications used in the DRI. Accordingly, nutrient intake
recommendations for the growth period were extrapolated from adult EAR or AI by
jointly accounting for metabolic body weight and growth factors, as described below.
Growth factors are 0.30 for 6–11 months and 1–2 years, 0.15 for 3–14 years, 0.15 for
males aged 15–18 years, and 0 for females aged 15–18 years.
For sodium, chloride, and potassium, increased physical activity leads to greater
electrolyte losses through sweat; therefore, EER rather than body weight were used in the
extrapolation equations. The EER applied in these calculations was the mean of the 2025
EER for adult men and women aged 19-29, 30-49, and 50-64 years.

무기질
When evidence required to establish ULs for growth period is insufficient, values were
derived by extrapolation from the ULs of another age group with the most similar
physiological and metabolic characteristics, with adjustment based on body-weight ratios.
However, when evidence for UL in age groups comparable to the target age group was
also lacking, values were extrapolated from the adult ULs.
(3) Older Adults
When evidence for older adults was limited, nutrient reference values were
extrapolated from adult values using metabolic body-weight scaling:
For sodium and chloride, however, extrapolation was instead based on EER, as energy
intake and expenditure decline with age:
In older adults, factors such as reduced chewing capacity, decreased digestion and
absorption efficiency, and lower physical activity commonly contribute to reduced food
intake, with wide inter-individual variability. Thus, when planning diets for older adults,
both chronological age and individual physiological conditions should be carefully
considered. Moreover, as many older adults live with chronic conditions, the conventional
DRI concept of health may require further reconsideration for this population in future
revisions.

General Outline
(4) Pregnancy and Lactation
For nutrients with sufficient evidence to establish an EAR or RNI, reference values for
pregnancy and lactation were determined by adding the incremental nutrient needs of
these physiological states to the corresponding non-pregnant or non-lactating values.
For example, the energy requirement for pregnancy includes the energy requirement of
maternal tissue growth and fetal development, while that for lactation accounts for the energy
needed for milk production, adjusted for the energy mobilized from maternal fat stores.
2-6. Nutrient Intake Status of Koreans
Data on nutrient intake status among Koreans are critically important when AI need to
be established based on population intake levels, particularly median intake [35]. Such
data are also essential for evaluating the extent to which the established dietary intakes
align with actual intakes levels and for assessing the practical applicability of the
standards for the development of 2025 KDRI, data from the KNHANESConsidering the
potential dietary changes during the COVID-19 pandemic (2020–2022), analyses were
conducted separately using datasets that included and excluded the pandemic period
(2020-2022). Accordingly, nutrient intakes from foods were evaluated based on three
datasets: the most recent 5 years (2018–2022), the most recent 10 years (2013–2022), and
the most recent 7 years excluding the COVID-19 period (2013–2019).
To estimate nutrient intakes from foods, a total of 30,421 participants from the 2018–
2022 KNHANES who completed both the health examination and nutrition survey were
initially considered. Among these, 23,382 individuals were excluded, including pregnant
or lactating women (n = 125), breastfed infants (n = 20), individuals with physician-
diagnosed diseases (n = 18,801), and those who reported atypical intake compared with
their usual diet (n = 8,436). As a result, 24-hour dietary recall data from 8,094 individuals
were included in the final analysis. The same exclusion criteria were applied to the
analyses of the most recent 10-year dataset (2013–2022) and 7-year dataset excluding the
COVID-19 pandemic period (2013–2019).
In the 2020 KDRI, nutrient intake levels were analyzed for a total of 21 nutrients. In
the present analysis, five additional nutrients—vitamin D, vitamin E, folate, magnesium,
and zinc—whose intake data have been available since the 7th KNHANES (2016–2018),
were included. Thus, intake analyses were conducted for a total of 26 nutrients: energy,
carbohydrates, dietary fiber, protein, fat, saturated fatty acids, monounsaturated fatty

무기질
acids, polyunsaturated fatty acids, omega-3 fatty acids, omega-6 fatty acids, cholesterol,
vitamin A, vitamin D, vitamin E, vitamin C, thiamin, riboflavin, niacin, folate, calcium,
phosphorus, sodium, potassium, magnesium, iron, and zinc. For individuals aged ≥1
year, mean and median intakes from foods were calculated by sex and age group, along
with intake distribution characteristics based on percentile analyses. In addition, the
proportions of individuals consuming less than the EAR or exceeding the UL were
estimated.
To assess nutrient intakes from dietary supplements, 2018–2021 KNHANES data (n =
25,959) were analyzed, excluding 2022 due to the unavailability of supplement intake
data for that year. Participants were categorized as supplement users or non-users based
on supplement use, and additional subgroup analyses were conducted based on whether
supplements containing the specific nutrient of interest were consumed. A total of eight
nutrients—vitamin A, vitamin C, thiamin, riboflavin, niacin, calcium, phosphorus, and
iron—were analyzed. For these nutrients, mean and median intakes and intake
distribution from foods alone, dietary supplements alone, and foods plus supplements
were evaluated. Furthermore, the proportions of individuals consuming less than the EAR
or exceeding the UL were calculated separately for each intake source category. These
analytical results were used as reference data for establishing dietary reference intakes
for energy and nutrients and for evaluating feasibility of the standards by comparison
with actual intake levels.
2-7. Major Food Sources
Because nutrients enter the body through food consumption, identifying which foods
contain specific nutrients and determining the major dietary sources commonly
consumed by Koreans are essential steps for assessing nutrient intake status and for
establishing dietary reference intakes that are applicable in real-world settings. Even for
the same nutrient, its content and bioavailability may vary depending on the type of food.
Thus, a food-based approach is important for the qualitative evaluation of nutrient intake
and for generating evidence to support rational dietary reference intake settings [36].
Data from the 2022 KNHANES raw dataset [37] and the National Standard Food
Composition Table DB 10.2 [38] were used to systematically estimate the contribution of
foods to nutrient intakes and to identify major dietary sources among Koreans. First, the
average intake of each food was calculated based on the KNHANES third-level food

General Outline
codes. These values were then linked with nutrient content per 100 g from the National
Standard Food Composition Table to calculate nutrient intakes by individual foods. Based
on these estimates, the contribution percentages of each food to total nutrient intake was
calculated, and the top 30 major food sources were identified. The resulted rankings were
compared with previously reported source food rankings presented in the 2022 Korea
Health Statistics [18] to assess validity.
However, previous analysis approaches provided only rankings of food sources along
with nutrient content per 100 g, making it difficult to clearly interpret the actual
contribution of foods that are consumed in large amounts but have relatively low nutrient
density, or conversely, foods that are consumed in small amount but have high nutrient
density. To address this limitation, total nutrient intake contributed by all major food
sources for each nutrient was first summed, and the proportional contribution of each
food to the total nutrient intake was then expressed as a percentage and presented
together. This approach allows for a more intuitive understanding of the extent to which
nutrients are actually obtained from specific foods.
For the identified major food sources, serving sized proposed in the 2020 KDRI
utilization study [39] were applied to estimate the amount of nutrients provided by serving.
These values were then compared with the RNI or AI for adults aged 19–29 years, as
presented in the 2020 KDRI [12], and visualized in graphs. This approach enables a clear
visualization of how much of the recommended or adequate intake is met by consuming
a single serving of each food, thereby providing practical reference information to
support food choices among the general population.
To overcome the limitations of the previous approaches that identified high-nutrient-
density foods based solely on nutrient content per 100 g across all foods, a revised
method was adopted in which high-nutrient foods were ranked based on frequently
consumed foods within the top 200 by consumption. Under the previous approach, foods
that are not frequently consumed in daily diet-such as dried lily roots, Phellinus linteus
mushrooms, and flaxseeds—could appear at the top of the rankings simply because of
their high nutrient density per 100 g, despite their low habitual intake. To improve
relevance, the present analysis identified high-nutrient foods specifically among
frequently consumed foods. Moreover, to address the potential exclusion of food groups
such as fish, shellfish, and seaweeds, which may have relatively low consumption
amounts but contribute significantly to certain nutrients, the analytical scope was
expanded to include the top 200 consumed foods rather than limiting the analysis to the

무기질
top 100. For nutrients such as vitamin D, for which the range of consumable food
sources is relatively limited, high-nutrient foods were identified from all foods rather
than restricting the analysis to frequently consumed items.
This major food source analysis was conducted to comprehensively assess nutrient
intake from foods among Koreans, providing fundamental data that consider not only the
quantitative level of intake but also its qualitative contribution. The findings are intended
to inform the establishment of more practical and applicable dietary reference values.
3 Update Summary of 2025 KDRI
The 2025 KDRI provide intake standards for a total of 41 nutrients, including 12 energy
and macronutrient components, 13 vitamins, 15 minerals, and the newly established
vitamin-like nutrient, choline. In the 2025 KDRI revision, the lower bounds of the AMDR
for carbohydrates and protein were modified: the AMDR for carbohydrates was changed
from 55-65% to 50-65% of total energy, and that for protein was from 7-20% to 10-20%
of total energy. The EARs and/or RNIs were revised for carbohydrate, protein, amino
acids, vitamin A, riboflavin, niacin, folate, calcium, phosphorus, magnesium, iron,
copper, iodine, and molybdenum. AIs were revised for dietary fiber, linoleic acid,
alpha-linolenic acid, EPA+DHA, pantothenic acid, sodium/chloride, potassium, fluoride,
and chromium. In addition, ULs were revised for niacin, vitamin B , calcium, magnesium,
fluoride, and molybdenum, and the CDRR for sodium was also updated.
The 2025 KDRI Committee conducted a systematic review for choline, which was
considered to require establishment of DRI in 2020. —After re-evaluating its necessity and
the level of scientific evidence, AI and UL values for choline were newly established.
Following promulgation by the Ministry of Health and Welfare, the 2025 KDRI will be
utilized across various sectors, including central government policies, educational settings,
and the food industry. Based on the data from Korea National Health and Nutrition
Examination Survey and ongoing monitoring results, the need for further refinement and
revision of the standards will be reassessed in the future (Figure 3).

4 Applications
The Dietary Reference Intakes for Koreans (KDRI) are designed to promote public
health and prevent disease by providing as a scientific basis for assessing the adequacy
of dietary intake and for planning diets at both the individual and population levels. In
addition, the KDRI provide the foundation for a wide range of national nutrition policies,
including the National Dietary Guidelines for Koreans, the Health Plan 2030, and the

무기질
establishment of Nutrient Reference Values used in nutrition labeling, as well as for
initiatives related to dietary improvement, meal service management, and other nutrition
and health-related programs.
The following section outlines the application of the 2025 revised KDRI to dietary
assessment and diet planning. Resources including 「Food-based Dietary Patterns」,
「Recommended Meal Plans」, the 「Food Balance Wheel」, and teaching–learning materials
based on the 2025 KDRI will be released in a separate publication, Application of the
2025 KDRI.
(1) Dietary Assessment
Dietary assessment evaluates whether a diet's quantity and quality are appropriate, and
determines which DRI value is applicable based on the nutrient characteristics and
assessment purpose. Because habitual intake is difficult to measure accurately, dietary
intake survey data are used to estimate the likelihood of nutrient adequacy when
applying the KDRI to individuals or groups.
❙Figure 4❙ Use of DRIs in Individual and Group Meal Planning [39]
For individual dietary assessment, the EAR, RNI, or AI may be applied; intakes below
the EAR indicate a high risk of inadequacy, while intakes at or above the RNI or AI
suggest low risk. Reference values are based on healthy individuals, and adjustments may
be required for specific medical conditions or physiological needs.
For groups, the proportion of individuals with intakes below the EAR can be estimated
to identify nutrients likely to be inadequate and the extent of insufficiency. Reference
values are based on healthy individuals, and adjustments may be required for specific
medical conditions or physiological needs. It should be emphasized, however, that the
RNI is not used to assess dietary adequacy at the group level.
For both individuals and groups, exceeding the UL indicates a high likelihood of
adverse effects due to excessive intake. When evaluating intakes against the UL, it is

General Outline
important to account for nutrient consumption from supplements, including fortified
foods, health functional foods. Since the basis for the UL varies by nutrient, the specific
criteria applied should always be confirmed. For example, niacin has separate ULs for
nicotinic acid and nicotinamide, while the ULs for folic acid and magnesium apply only
to intakes from supplements or fortified products.
(2) Dietary Planning
The goal of dietary planning is to ensure that individuals and populations promote and
maintain health by providing adequate nutrition. Dietary planning based on the DRIs is
applied in various settings, including institutional food services (e.g., schools and
workplaces), government food assistance programs, dietary guidelines, and food product
development standards (Table 7).
For individual dietary planning, the goal is to achieve intakes close to the RNI or AI
levels. Energy requirements are first estimated using the EER equation, and energy should
then be distributed according to the Acceptable Macronutrient Distribution Ranges
(AMDR) for carbohydrates, protein, and lipid. For micronutrients, nutrient intakes should
be planned to meet the RNI or AI without exceeding the UL. Nutrient priorities may be
set based on an individual’s specific nutritional issues. Since the KDRI is developed for
healthy individuals, adjustments may be necessary based on body weight, health status, or
other individual characteristics.
For group dietary planning, the objective is to minimize the proportion of individuals
whose nutrient intakes fall below the EAR or exceed the UL. Accordingly, dietary plans
for healthy populations should be designed so that the group’s average energy intake
meets the mean EER, while macronutrient energy distribution falls within the AMDR,
ensuring a balanced intake of carbohydrates, protein, and fat, and micronutrient intakes
meet or exceed the RNI or AI without surpassing the UL.
Before planning, dietary intake data should be analyzed to determine the proportion of
individuals with intakes below the EAR, which informs nutrient improvement goals and
guides the planning process. Even when meals are designed according to these criteria,
actual consumption may vary; therefore, post-implementation evaluation and adjustment
of dietary plans are essential to effectively address nutritional issues.

2025 Dietary Reference Intakes for Koreans – Sugars
Ministry of Health and Welfare, Republic of Korea, 2025
Total sugar intake should not exceed 20% of total energy intake, and in particular, added
sugars incorporated during food preparation and processing should be restricted to less than
10% of total energy intake. Added sugars include sugar, high-fructose corn syrup, starch
syrup, molasses, honey, syrups, and concentrated fruit juice. In particular, consumption of
sugar-sweetened beverages, which are major sources of added sugars, should be minimized.
∙ EER: estimated energy requirements ∙ EAR: estimated average requirement
∙ AI: adequate intake ∙ UL: tolerable upper intake level
∙ RNI: recommended nutrient intake

2020 한국인 영양소 섭취기준 – 당류
보건복지부, 2020
총당류 섭취량을 총 에너지섭취량의 10-20%로 제한하고, 특히 식품의 조리 및 가공 시 첨가되는 첨가당은 총 에너지섭취량
의 10% 이내로 섭취하도록 한다. 첨가당의 주요 급원으로는 설탕, 액상과당, 물엿, 당밀, 꿀, 시럽, 농축과일주스 등이 있다.
